People generally bullish about MCP, moreso than I expected

Bearish about embeddings / vector databases, and about open models.

I'll dig out the figure, about supposedly enterprise LLM calls are 5% open models and falling.  I suppose product owners generally want the best and that this overrides data questions in an overwhelming majority of cases.

Those who did burn time training their own models said they had little to show for it in terms of useful LLMs, but that the skills and processes were extremely valuable afterwards

Supposedly we are some way from making real inroads on "replace the SRE", effectively stuck at "anomaly detection, but via LLM" stage so far

Favourable reviews of Gemini 2.5 Pro across the board

General sense that Claude Code outperforms other LLM developer tools for same tasks /w same models, but it's not clear to me yet why this might be, maybe 3.7 & CC were developed to suit one another, unsure.

Reading back on your stuff, agree that 'data lake' is costly (complication, caching), and ultimately solves a redundant human problem around wrangling things - ideally just give me read access and let us build the MCPs to get what we need from source.

At the very least we should attempt this first, and only add complication once we hit problems (unreliable outputs might take us towards 'data lake' / standardisation layer / etc)

So, could we start small now? Hook up to 1-2 key sources, minimum viable MCP server.  Get felt sense for feasibility by kicking things, probably don't worry much about presentation layer right away.

Wondering how MCP servers would break down, whether it's one per source, or one per format, or whatever.  Partly comes down to the detail of how quickly MCP falls over, and the complication of individual sources / range of sources in play.  I'm guessing we've got all kinds of formats to consume, and you mentioned unstructured data of various sorts that is subject to change bc hand-edited.

Robert Kirby
Bearish about embeddings / vector databases, and about open models.      I'll dig out the figure, about supposedly enterprise LLM calls are 5% open models and falling.  I suppose product owners generally want the best and that this overrides data questions in an overwhelming majority of cases.     …
I personally find vector databases as limited value. In many ways, almost like data lakes. Since the vector db, and the data lack are a fake source of truth that is very hard to actually maintain

Robert Kirby
Sorry, OpenAPI, misread you
I mis-typed. I originally had OpenAI

Yes agree, plus postgres does it all already, stay away from bespoke / lock-in

But AFAICT most interest in vector DBs was kind of a category error, conflating it with RAG

ya, RAG is "Retrival" augmented generation, its retriving data in any form. Vector DBs are just a flavor of it

in effect a special very expensive index

something for this week for us to work out though is if data lakes are dead. I believe they are.

1) They exist to help people navigate a range of data stores ----- well, the AI's can do that

And people thought they needed to retrieve distributed context when actually vector DBs are more about associative search

2) They exist to expose a way to create a unified view of data with access controls ----- agents can do this as well as they query multiple sources.

3) Datalakes do not allow people to edit hte content. To take action on it. So you still actually need to hook up to all the end systems anyway. So your basicalyl doing double the work for little value

This is why in aleandria I just integrated with zendesk, salesforce, etc directly

because I could load everything into a vector db, but then the vector db is just a slow, buggy, cache of data

Its only of value if the APIs to the application I need to integrate with are broken with respect to search and requireme me to add search

and this is why MCP is such a big deal, it allows you to rapidly integrate with those systems with off the shelf integrations

MCP gives people a way to bypass vectordb caches that so many products have been built on so far. Glean for example is obsolete if MCP excels

BTW keep hearng battle-scarred people say "resist rolling your own X until you have exhausted pre-baked solutions" and people like Swyx nodding sagely along

Where X is things like - models, fine tunes, enterprise data solutions, embedding pipelines, orchestration layers, data ingestion frameworks, vector indexing, etc etc etc
And clearly in some cases we will disagree, but I do think worth "devil's advocate stage" in each case, as I reckon it'll take minutes now with search tools being as good as they are, and with the best people on podcasts & transcripts /YT as text available to Gemini.

Glean is basically an AI data lake

Robert Kirby

agreed. Its expensive. And most people "build it and they will come"

(Ofc 'model /w search' / 'deep research' no stand-in for taste, but gives us something to react to to iterate quick)

the parts I'm doing are teh low cost areas

and even then, a bunch of it may rapidly become obsolete

this is why I've avoided embeddings, fine tuning, rolling our own models, etc.

Illumex interesting but much bigger risk as it's enterprise-level commitment.  Their founder is very open about their approach, though

supporting multiple diverse agents is still novel. But realistically that is a small trick nobody has realized yet how to do. But I doubt it will be novel by the end of the year

* <2025-03-28 Fri>

*Brainstorming session* /w IR
Very loose chat about Amazon Q, penny drop moment for me about how useful it is.

STT about it here:
Ian and I spoke for probably about an hour and the main topic was about how Amazon Q Developer is an insanely Versatile tool that might well cover the workflow stuff that we've been dreaming of so far it's basically Claude code booked for free because we have a deal with our AWS rep.  They've given us unlimited tokens for a kind of trial to get us hooked as an organisation. An interesting thing about the tool is that it Comes with caveats about the number of tokens you can send, but in fact this is just a limitation on how many it can send to the anthropic API and actually Claude isn't limited in the same way it can be the full context window and so you aren't really limited in practice in the size of the context window of the LLM. This is a massive massive deal because it means we effectively have free Claude code. And CC is expensive.

The other thing is that the branding Claude code was wrong from the very beginning because it channels you into thinking in terms of software development environments when actually it is a Terminal based tool that can call all sorts of tools such as curl FFmpeg git, As well as writing to files. It's basically a holistic computing environment with an agent frontend. You give Amazon Q The keys to your local environment, so to speak, And then you can just tell it to make stuff so had an awful lot of the stuff you wish you could do on the Terminal you don't do because of friendliness rather than because it's actually complex to do so for instance I wrote a bash script on the call for curl request requests to our azure instance, And then realised that although I never test bash this is just because of the fiddliness Of the practice of testing bash.  No such problem for Amazon Q so we just wrote the tests.  You are literally a prompt away from what you need most of the time on the terminal, Whereas before you would have been reliant on the grey beard sysadmin to get many things done.The models just get bash.

Ian is very keen to set up training sessions that have us demo various aspects of process using Amazon Q. He had the idea of a session focused on effectively what were formally understood to be product sprints like you might use Q to devise an epic and then device tasks which would historically be tickets and then use Q to suck down the tickets into your local environment and provide them as a prompt to Q itself to write the code. And code review and tests and and and etc. Q to push to the remote repo. (And a separate suggestion from me, not part of the conversation but thinking of it now: perhaps several instances of q doing this at once using git-worktree.)

Ian and I also had a separate conversation about our working styles because Chris did ask us to come together and chat about what we learnt about working practices during the Phantom demo prep.  Ian pointed out that he is future oriented in his disposition and also has trouble switching gears which for him is both a strength especially given the current pace of change in LLMs and the fact that his role is focused on it but it's also a weakness in his view because it means he cannot switch personas so to speakin order to fulfil a wide range of tasks because he will operate fast slower on the axis that matter matters the most right now namely research and development and conceptualising future use cases for the business as a whole.  On the other hand, I have no trouble flitting in fact I happen to love it. I also have a strong preference for software that just works which means I am very well placed to operate in the make it so category of tasks and more general coddling together of things for deadlines; I could easily see us in a situation where Ian provides vision and I make stuff happen while providing a sanity check on it, Maybe something like pairing of staff dev and technical project manager. Just as an aside, this wasn't in the conversation, but I am very very keen to get my hand in on recruiting and training work around this stuff.

Actually, from the conversation and related to that final point. Ian pointed out that we are both relatively unusual as software engineers in being able to grok the implications of all the LLM stuff at the moment. In particular many of the people that we are following quite closely are relatively focused on incremental releases and the implications for coding.  Neither Ian or I are as focused on coding we see it is just a subset of the product development process and we are kind of astonished that the rest of the world isn't thinking in these terms in an obvious way. Perhaps Amjad Masad is an exception, but from him, I see a lot of motivated reasoning and a suite of products that we would never use in our situation as a business.

This is implications for VBUs Because the rank-and-file software engine is there are generally in a form of learned helplessness around the sprint process some of them will definitely see their identity is bound up and being line engineers so to speak effectively taking tickets and turning them into completed tasks via editing code with a little bit of clarifying requirements here and there but otherwise staying largely well within the domain of tax editing and software engineering and kind of dislocated from product. This absolutely has to change. There's also the point that the same people will probably have expectations around systems being deterministic.  There will be some degree of change we can bring about here just through talking and training but they will almost certainly be some who do not make the leap towards the kind of right hemispheric focus that is required to get the best out of the current LLMs.

There are also obvious implications for hiring that we need to think through. So far this week I've been hopping on about how the next form of developer will almost certainly look very different to the previous sort because we have selected for analytical left brain nerdy types and actually that won't fly so well in the face of LLMs that can do all the fiddly things And faster. However, I don't think it's enough to be good at talking to models. You need to be someone who can get the joke about how the text editor is now redundant that coding is now an impediment to product development and software engineering. So we have to be careful because we both have the challenge of getting a good felt sense of what the new ideal hire looks like and the Distribution of personality types that sit within the ideal range, But we do also then have to consider that people who intuitively understand our lambs may not be the same people who intuitively understand product at least not already.  So there may be an open question of arbitrage around people who are not in the middle ground of the Venn diagram so to speak.

Chris Wildsmith asked Ian and I to get together and talk about lessons learned from the previous project and I guess that the last few paragraphs do capture this the Amazon Q stuff is more recent and as implications for what we will do in the coming quarter I actually suspect that currently team's plans And the emphasis of those plants don't currently reflect the implications of widespread Amazon queue usage across the business. But we can get to that. It's a discussion to be had with Chris,And I think it will naturally fall out of ordinary conversation.

One thing I totally forgot to mention here was that Ian and I realised that the 'read eval print loop' Of writing software has vastly changed so like aside from the fact you were using agents to write the codes there is this interesting problem of waiting that now permeate everything we do I do suspect that there is an awfully strong case for pairing here because direction that is so much more than speed given the speed is converging on infinity. Also, as a separate point we should be recording literally every meeting all of this stuff is fodder for LLMs.  Every summary we have right is a demonstratable waste of time because LLMs can do a better job than us and quicker. Any meeting we do not record as a lost opportunity because any context that we lose is context, we will never get back. You could easily discard what you have. And the models can read extremely well over long context windows and leave out the bits you don't need.

Three things that CW wants in next 6 weeks or so WRT Phantom:
- 'Dynamic business logic' - can we mess with the *scope* of what the LLM can choose from tools, give feedback to user about what tools / tasks are in fact currently possible etc.  [Perhaps a means of asking for a new feature / tool via the LLM].
- One idea - we have a lease in the system, the tenant puts in a challenge about their lease agreement, say about what it says about animals in properties, contesting something.  - This is the kind of situation where you might go to a lawyer. Really want to have AI provide courses of action - do you want me to be conciliatary, legally adversarial, etc etc; the AI should show its chain of reasoning in the chat with the landlord.
- Mobile app up and running. [It wouldn't surprise me if CW expected this to be the flagship UI for future demos.]

IR I'm trying to do something simple that can handle light / dark themes.  Bright primary colour banners rarely have dark / light opposites.   And, since want to suport both, am takign a leaf out of Microsoft UIs book - literally looking at them and seeing how they do colour, icons etc since they do support light/dark modes. * <2025-03-06 Thu>
*Project Phantom Working Meeting* - CW JC NS DD IR SS
JC Grosvenor haven't replied to SS (jokes, wonder if they even do business)
It's a nice to have, but ultimately the property management business is the sort of thing we can figure out without talking to Grosvenor.
Perhaps Rob[? not Kirby] could ask them if they've had any leads to prompt them into checking their inbox.

SS this week I spoke to a sales director from Yardi and someone from MRI.  I'll go over my insights with you shortly.
Ian and Neil have been going over the Buildium user flow to figure out next steps.
In terms of customer demo, I haven't heard back from Grosvenor yet. I will continue to reach out over email.
And JC was also suggesting using Rob [not Kirby] to get a demo.

CW That might blow cover, so maybe not.
Here in the meetings with Yonder we've discussed how Customer Intimacy is so important.
So if Grosvenor aren't replying to customer messages, we can highlight that when we come to present things at the end of this project.
Like maybe make a phonecall (if it's even an option), but don't go mad as we don't want to break cover by harrassing them.
JC: SS please note lack of response for later.

CW Good at this point to note unusual elements.
Ian's "oil change" [see email chain, basically progress made using agentic workflows while doing other things] was one. The lack of response now is another.
We could call things like this "moments of truth", separate to the things we can prove by copycatting their project using AI.
I.e. aside from the software development process, we can talk about these things when we come to present to Grosvenor about Project Phantom.
Oil change thing is an example of "Intelligent Trust" - you are offloading work but keeping oversight of AI agents.

SS MRI customer told me how system works.  Told me that it's clunky system, property management system is bad.  He's been around in the industry for 25 years. But there's still a big need for a system that does good property management but it _user friendly_.
CW That's similar to my experience in my startup years ago, where we displaced a rental management system for universities, which was horrible to use & people therefore didn't trust.
I just wonder - NS - you have UI / UX passion; we should perhaps elevate usability when we demonstrate things.  Perhaps voice interface for UX is going to be very high payoff.

CW How does MRI and Yardi compared to Buildium?
SS MRI & Yardi have gotten the financial management system part just right.  When it comes to usability, the systems can get so complex that people end up having to rely on Excel.
So 2 problems - usability is bad, but also customers are piecing things togetherfrom different systems.
CW A good example is what Xero have done for accounting - human first presentation and UX, rather than system first.  Visible, friendly way of presenting.
[JC shows Yardi UI - it basically looks like a table, not user-friendly.]
JC Don't over index on this, but - is mocking up Buildium a better idea than Grosvenor?
CW I think NS should arbitrate on that. It's possible to make UI 'nice' while UX is still bad.  We won't know for sure, because we aren't the customer. Making it easy to interrogate data without having to understand the system is so powerful.
So at least *saying* we understand UX and having this as a wildcard "shock & awe" feature would be smart.

IR Property management / identity management usability issues are so similar [IR has security background].
CW Previous work with property management showed that easy UX was unbelievably high payoff in making users actually like / trust the product.

NS My experience looking at various websites. Users complain that complexity has rocketed but the usability has been left behind.
The older generation (many landlords) are struggling.  Too many clicks needed, dropdowns involved, options.
We should consider sorting this out.

SS I think Buildium, looking at their website, it's relatively straightforward to use.  Generally similar setup to Grosvenor, but perhaps a better baseline.

CW Yes as a baseline.  I'd love to challenge 1x UX convention of Grosvenor's product.  Once the baseline product is built, as a nice to have.
JC Yes after the baseline is met.

IR So to simplify down how you manage a billion dollar portfolio project from your car.
CW Yes, in this business your focus should be on how your users feel when they get things done with your system.
IR Yes so diminishing returns on additional features, complexity costs you UX.

SS For workflows, critical workflows don't change too much for asset types like residential offices etc.  Generally companies have chosen to have in-house accounting option so they can offer a comprehensive solution, rather than using Xero.
Self service solutions also a requirement - for tenants, vendors, landlords etc.  So on top of basic workflows through property management, rent etc you will want a sales / rental portal for rent applications.
JC And if self service portals are so important, maybe this is the best place to demonstrate a step ahead from traditional forms.
RK I don't know what a robust solution would look like if I'm honest, but maybe robustness less important in lead generation.  We should look for other such cases as we go along.  Tolerance to noise, lossiness, robustness etc are good use cases for AI features in the product.

JC last time I rented, I went on Kagigi[? craigslist competitor] and I basically just DM'd someone.  It was kind of sketchy as a process.
CW There are a lot of headaches

SS I asked providers in this space like Yardi.  They said they considered AI to be hype.  They said it's an old industry and that the incumbents / landlords will be happy to continue as before.
Chatbots are kind of common for basic interaction with the tenant or earlier in the marketing time to stream tenants.  Relatively simplistic.  And used around reporting / forecasting.
There's no clearly defined AI use casea that already have a clear commercial impact.  They're still in the early stage.
CW Yes and this is something we need to track.  20 years ago we were better going out and seeing the customer.  Don't Make Me Think style.  You don't see it these days.  We could do this with the product.  We could, for instance show a user the forecasting tool if we were to make one and see what they say.
JC Projections on market, recommendations to save, projections, forecasting on rental market etc.  It's a stretch goal perhaps.
CW Yes keep building up a list of ideas.  We need a baseline but more the merrier for ideas we can pick off after we achieve a basic product.

SS Preventative maintainance also potentially quite powerful, spoke to IR about this.
IR Yes. So how do we do a decent 20 min demo at the end. I've 2 ideas right now.  We want to 'shock & awe' Grosvenor.
One idea is to show a property management workflow - log in, see their portfolio, some indication of expected profitability over next 3 years; then tent can log in and submit maintainance request; property manager goes in their portal and assigns a vendor; the vendor goes in and fixes things and we seek a vendor using an app to update the landlord; it shows up in the landlord's view.
JC Could we automate some of the landlord decisionmaking - perhaps tenant picture is automatically sent to the preferred vendor.
RK Perhaps landlord opts in to this flow ahead of time
JC And then landlord notified, not required to give input.

CW I showed an app a leaking windscreen a few years ago.  It took 2 pictures and then told me whether the work needed doing.

IR Forms are tedius, especially on mobile.  A voice / chat agent could be the way here.  It asks questions until satisfied.  It's easy to implement.
So... Perhaps we should get the mobile app up and running.  We have a basica admin UI up and running.
SS Yes so Ian has a basic UI up and running.  Lease info, property info, tenant info all displayed.
IR The AI agents are stamping out features really fast now. The UI is mostly just web forms, and we could even use an Excel table potentially.  It's not hard to provide.
JC Buildium presents a table.
IR No, they do forms.  They generally put it all on a single page.  The form structure is just a default I chose.
JC The fundamentals of just adding a couple of records for property, tenant and lease is already there.
IR Yep.
JC And we'll be fudging fake data.
IR Yep.  Right now there's not business logic except anomaly detection, risk scoring etc on document uploads.
CW So no real business logic just yet.
IR Not yet.

IR Right now all IDs are random, so each unit has a random ID for its properties. I need to create a real simulated environment where all the IDs match up and therefore you can hyperlink in the UI.  It can be done next week.
If we were to segment this into the end user UI strategy, then we really get to focus on the end user workflow.
JC The basic workflows will need to look good.  People will challenge use with user flows when we demo the product on stage.
IR Let's say you want to add a user - you can already.  There's a 'wizard' that can walk users through user flow steps.
JC Yes but I really mean actions outside of the intended demo - it needs to survive interrogation on stage.
CW Yes so e.g. it should calculate rent accurately or whatever.

IR When we present, we'll want to talk about how we have a reusable web / mobile app platform to rapidly recreate these types of product.
We need to display a historical project plan like what o1 Pro generated for me.  2 months of planning, 6 months of getting infra in place etc.  We're basically cutting out those first 8 typical months here, and we should tell that story.
It will massively challenge expectations of engineering managers.
JC: SS please take note, we should have a slide for that kind of thing.
IR Yes so if we are asked what the time savings are, we can point to these as the kinds of things we can cut out.
Engineering teams will nitpick everything, so we will demonstrate that they can get real value quickly.

IR I have my opinions on how to estimate on the time it'd take to build, I'd be guessing - opinionated but uninformed.  Perhaps we could ask Yonder (software services company we are friendly with) for an estimate.
JC They'll ask for a project spec.
CW Maybe just ask for a PoC estimate, using zero AI assistance.
IR I'd be quoting a year or two for something like this.
NS Maybe the same?
IR It took Bravura Security's DevOps person 6 months to build infrastructure like the stuff I was able to build in 2 weeks.  And that DevOps person was good.

CW Yes maybe order of magnitude might be good enough estimate for non-AI process estimate.
RK And gotta consider resources, not just time - DB dev, ML dev, Mobile Dev, Web FE or full stack dev.  It's a team.
IR I can do it all on my own with state of the art LLMs.
RK Yes.

NS Note that many thousands of concurrent users is a harder class of problem.
CW Yes but you can present as POC.
IR And Grosvenor was using homespun infra before, which I wouldn't trust for that. Project Phantom is using Azure, which I'd trust way more.

JC Devs in our team plus someone from Yonder to provide non-AI workflow estimates for delivering this.

JC Aside, meeting next week with Kevin, Troy, Dwight where we'll talk about ValuePro by the way.

SS Maybe as next step we should lay out a scenario that walks people through a property management system.  So a scenario for AI and for the demo[?].

CW And maybe we should start wireframing or just listing out what will need to be in the whole presentation.
SS How long will we have to demo on March 26th?
CW About and hour.  They won't know why we're there.  There's other topics to cover beyond the demo.  Security, DevOps.
NS Quick question.  How many people in the demo will be in property management?
CW 60-70% of the audience, from approx 4 Volaris Business Units.

IR We can set the scope by saying we're looking at it from the perspective of a rentor.
CW Just to be careful, though.  It'll largely be an audience of people with commercial tenants.
IR But everyone can relate to (say) a leaky AC unit, so we can still demo that.
CW Yes but things like reviewing a lease are worth focussing on.
JC Their customers generally own blocks.
SS It's a mixture of residential and commercial.
CW In my conversation with Rob[?], he said commercial property managers are the people to focus on, since they feel the most pain.

NS We were talking earlier about the analytics side.  We realised this is so much more important to block managers.
CW Yes they want to understand their return, their vacancy rate, which blocks are proving unpopular, what his rent should be set to for the next year, etc etc.
They're people with 1 or 2 blocks, relatively unsophisticated but working hard to make money.
So someone on their phone in their car, making deals, with other things going on too.  Usually the customer is a solo property managers.

SS One of the customers from my research were commercial, often managing blocks for high net worth individuals.
CW Manufacturing, car servicing, some residential etc etc.  Retail.  It'll be a mixture, potentially with high churn and maintainance issues.

NS So what kind of data analytics / graphs will you want to see? This will help IR to understand what to collect.]

SS Customer said I need my board data first[?].  And if a property is to be sold I need to pull together all the information ASAP so I can get to market.  So having that info in one place is great. Forecasting rent would be great.

CW [Little rambly] This might be a third story for you to record, SS. People should be business problem owner, not product owners - we're hold to solve things, not to run systems.  Systems cause grief.  System focus ruins UX.  So going back to high level idea of the briefing.  Maybe a 1 minute intro about what we'll show.  Then a 15 min demo. 2 mins on what is there but isn't visible - security, our devops thinking etc.  But state they're there and we're using the right engineering approaches. Then we should say how we did it in 10 mins. RK to present 'levels' of AI thinking, the asymmetry of speed for start-ups amplified by AI software development processes.  Then, perhaps we start the demo.
So structure of talk:
Intro 1,

*Day 3 at Yonder AI visit* Many - JC JC FN X
- Huge asymmetry of payoff - new starters get a velocity boost, incumbents less so
- There's no magic, only trade-offs
- Three lenses, *must* have all three to get a meaningful assessment.  Team, project, AI capabilities.
- Incumbents need to experiment (heavyweight teams)
- Incumbents need to educate


- Team
  - Not developers, software professionals
  - Culture - awareness, experimentation, willingness
  - Skill
- Projects
- Tools
  - LLMs themselves
    - 5 stages, ending with IDE agents vs project agents
  - Systems through which LLMs are used
    - Copilot (indemnity, but stilted), Browser + Cursor (risky but quick)


For teams, where are you, where are the tools, where are you projects.  It might mean no change.

YCombinator - 95% - seniors at YC expect to see startups eat SAAS this year
Google - 25%
Legacy companies - 5%>?

So established businesses are exposed but can't move as fast.
Any startups limited by scale (complexity, difficulty, models, skill).
Perhaps less developed (i.e. less constrained) markets.


Then, in parallel you must run:
1. Experiments - Clay Christienson Heavyweight Teams, but also projects to paid customers, increasing complexity as you prove things
   - New initiatives
   - Product copycatting

2. Upskilling
   There is a broad level of basic awareness we can provide, but we need to put teams & projects into rough buckets using 'three lens' model to plan correctly.

   The 'levels' as per Paul Cirstean:
   0. No AI assistance
   2. Line completion
   3. Chat, considering suggestions and then manually editing
   4. Applying suggested edits across files using automatic workflow ('click here to apply')
   5. SWE agentic workflow - 'hands off' for minutes at a time as AI edits multiple files
   6. Product agentic workflow - from Jira ticket to 'done' with minimal human oversight


Key point that BAU improvements (maintainance) != prototyping / PoCs / Greenfield != product payoff

Tail risks
Process - 3 lenses
Business - product featues & design


- [ ] Draw up takeaways
  - [ ] Hackathons
  - [ ] White paper / point brief - initially for CW & JC, but bear in mind it'll go beyond (use notes from Paul / Remus session on 6th March)
  - [ ] 'Induction' - some form of general-purpose introduction to LLM for code, accepting that code isn't the full scope of our interest
  - [ ] "'Overclocking' is a better term than fine-tuning"

*Project Phantom - Working Meeting* /w SS NS IR DD JC CW

NS to give his impressions first.   He took some notes.  NS used an AI to look at the codebase a bit [?].

NS: We need to split into the shock & awe part, the voice stuff. The counter arguments - I spoke to a few devs in last couple days - they said it won't scale properly, you won't have good security etc.  So we need to confront the obvious objections.  So perhaps we should throw in some quality / hygiene checks rather tha just showing a toy app, so that we can make the point.  And lastly there needs to be some commercial success.
The three buckets:
1. Shock and Awe (neat feature, speed)
2. Deal With the Black Hats (preempt them)
3. We've done a neat commercial thing
The other thing I liked was the voice thing.  NS's wife told him there's a big need for WCAG (disabled compliance) in the UK.  So can the app speak to a person, a blind person or whatever.  This is a common complaint about UK property management apps.

IR: There are security vulnerabilities in all of the ValuePro files RK & I have looked at.  So just because it's your existing product, doesn't mean it's good.  Lots of code means larger surface area for this stuff.
Making sure we're compatible with WCAG checkers is vital too.  Microsoft has libraries that check for this stuff.

NS: One thing I thought of - does the library cover for multiple languages?

IR: We could activate that quickly.  It's not hard to activate.

NS: Asking because, imagine you're doing the demo, you show the app, you show them the language changing - shock and awe achieved.

CW: Yes.. We maybe need to innovate, estimate etc for things so that we can decide what we are / aren't going to go for in the alloted time.  Since there are the usual development pitfalls for development schedules.

IR: I like NS's 3 buckets of priorities.  So a primary goal in each bucket and then secondary buckets.  So then we can prioritise.  E.g. Security is no-brainer.  We can use Azure reports and choose scanners to put the code through.  Then for the accessibility layer - it'll matter a lot for people in the right industry vertical, because it's a commercial requirement for some.  So worth asking whether a11y is a commercial top priority for property companies - if it is, we should do it.  But it'll be a second / third week thing, bc security needs doing first for that bucket.

JC: Also worth stating we don't have to scale this up, it's a POC.  It's to say here's what we could demo as a potential next step for after March.  We need them to believe enough that they think we did make something.

CW: I like IR's model of how if we get the core elements done with lesser tasks beyond them - triage, basically.

JC: And we're looking at ValuePro so far, but we'll be porting over the Phantom?

IR: That's right.  And I'll be assigning out bugfix tickets to people.
[Shows UI, localhost:5173]
I'll bring a bunch of tools over to Phantom.

IR: Different - ValuePro do property valuations, estimations; Phantom will be more focussed on property management, handling mainainance requests etc.  There are similarities, and some people might own both types of software.

CW: So when you stand up that environment, do we already have a baseline of security and infrastructure?

CW: Great so SS please be the coordinator of what we're aiming for. So important to understand what we're starting with as prior work.  We start with a DevOps environment that comes from a sort of factory approach, no reinventing the wheel etc.  So from day 1 it has ability for multi-tenancy, core infrastructural ability, etc.
And then we have these 20 buckets horizontally, each one of which has a 'small'/'medium'/'large' rating.
At this point not too concerned if they aren't fully scoped.
But a sort of product management view, that'd be nice.

JC: The wiki pages do kinda do this, but I guess we want to categorise them and make it clear.

CW: Yes, the estimate part is paramount so we can plan.

IR: It's kind of like how a modern car is built.  Most have the same engine, drive train etc but they have different interiors, bodies etc.  That's what we do here.  So the same common infrastructure and core code, but tailored look and feel, and specific functionality. So people can still customise it, but we can releverage stuff.  E.g. Xero integration was written to be reusable.

IR: This is why so many apps which rely form submission are in so much trouble.

JC: That's a lot of businesses, and a lot of VG businesses!

NS: Did you come across anything impressive as you looked around at different software products?  Did anything pop out as different in a positive way?

CW: I just think the software world has become lazy!  We just follow the same patterns.  Years ago my company was developing systems for people to make enquiries, bookings etc.  And it might happen via agencies and then cancel.  They might want to quickly review their bookings.  But we didn't do that.  Whenever they had a company name, we put a smiley / happy face next to it, and people bizarrely *loved* this because it was a human touch.  So even if we're bringing in simple quality of life stuff like news feeds for property trends in that area or whatever - just try.  Users are so bored of boring forms!  There's no creativity and a little goes so far these days.  It's so easy to fall into that trap and make standard forms.

JC: We [SS & I] will go out there, talk to experts and see what they regard as important and standing out.

CW: Yes but just to reiterate - users love human touches in apps, and devs underrate this.

NS: If you have someone in the room who is an expert, you can critique it fast.  But if it's something odd that's not in their wheelhouse then you can put them on the back foot and even charm them somewhat.

IR: Thinking about legal now.  I assume property companies must have all kinds of problems.  RAG or PDF into context window - you can imagine big context models highlighting market specific concerns.  It's so easy these days.

So a lot of the architecting was Human driven but AI augemented.  And then the output is a list of detailed tasks, effectively specifications.  You hand this off to an AI coding tool, and monitor progress and quality.  Crudely speaking.

IR remains on call...

Alexandria is the main line of work.  It's the oldest stream of work for AI.  The current Azure DevOps version of the repo was put up a month ago.  Bravura Security originally owned this stuff (IR's company).  As IR / CW / JC talked about potential for Alexandria, they realised they wanted a broader tool.  ValuePro is this.

Volaris is an off-shoot of Alexandria.  Want a strict firewall between Volaris and Alexandria because of financial data.

Hierarchy:
- Alexandria brings in 95% of the infrastructre for the repos.
- Ainsworth & Volaris bring in config specific to their needs.  They're the customisation layer on top of Alexandria core.
"Think of Alexandria as my equivalent to Supabase, it brings in a stock set of configurations so that I can stamp out other flavours of it."

[IR setting up subscriptions]
Volaris entitlement 7 is IR's play area.
Volaris entitlement 8 is IR's intended production environment.  He's using Terraform.
Volaris entitlement 9 is IR's intended finance related area (probably Volaris).

Ainsworth will be in area 8.
Volaris will be in area 9.

Volaris are about to enter some AWS agreements, but Azure is the norm.

IR: AIs are really good at working with Azure services via the CLI.  AI agents are really good at troubleshooting issues with Azure.

IR: cosmos db is a nosql db.  Originally used SQL db, but IR thought if I need a reusable db that can be rapidly be applied to different environments, would rather ride by seat of pants and see what we can do.
Azure functions area is where IR is building out API layers.  It's hard to debug, says IR.  2 weeks ago had things running fine but almost lost a full week to troubleshooting a problem.  It's temperamental, be cautious with it.
When things go into the cosmos database, IR indexes it into Azure's semantic search DB.
SignalR is a way of doing websockets and serverside events.
The main are of the web application is known as '*-static-app'

The SignalR service can sometimes 'bounce itself'. [?]

IR: The way to think of the Volaris Project is that the underlying infrastructure is Alexandria.  Volaris Project should be no-code.

When users have a query, an under-the-hood initial LLM call decides which specialist persona to use.

*VG intro* /w CW
'Constellation' is this 'ball thing'.  I (RK) will meet some Constellation people in Romania.

Volaris & Harris are seen as the 'biggest' [within Constellation?].

'Persius' was very small until 2yrs ago but did a huge deal with mortgage broking business.  They're US centric, won't hear much from them.

'Topicus' - above Yonder.

Usually people stay in their 'swim lanes'.

There is a small culture difference between Constellation & VG. (Not specified.)

Standardised approach to calculating P&L - for legibility, to create collaborative / competitive culture.

Businesses kind of federal - are allowed to run independently.  When there are challenges / issues, they draw more attention.

CW: we aim to develop people "from below".  Decentralised.

We tend to work on projects / themes.

CW & Jeff Chow (JC) just been working on business in Boston.  Case study - didn't tell them what to do, instead show them what others do, coach them onto the right answer.

"It's a very 'investor' mindset. 'What's the return?'"
This applies to AI, too - questions naturally follow these lines from the starts.

NB 'lead developer' ~= CTO in VG.

CW introduces e.g. 'Portfolio' company - banking for niche market. They provide core banking capabilities.  Fallen behind.  They aren't getting a lot from their existing customers.  We did research on their existing customers, generally were unhappy, didn't feel product was evolving as fast as other providers.  Spent a lot of money replatforming to the cloud (negative vibes from VW).  The CEO of this business said of AI tools / coding assistance, we're using, getting minor efficiency gains.

CW hears more and more of people reporting using AI but not seeing it reflected in the productivity statistics.

CW: "One of the hype bubbles I'd like to burst - 'nibbles' on productivity gains of 2% here and there aren't going to do it".  CW thinks this is probably performative.

There is absolutely a desire to *measure the improvements* we get from AI, because we want to be genuinely competitive.

[This is totally correct - we're not here to maximise SAAS subscriptions, the point is to win.]

Don't want to be locally competitive - want to win in global markets, so we want to reach non-local maximums.

If you ever try to buy a business and say it's "for strategic reasons", you'll probably get laughed out of the room [but why?]

Generally you get people either highly competitive but zero sum, or collaborative but overly relaxed.  In the middle is where you find excellence.

A Swiss business that is a great example of this form of excellence.  Were going bankrupt when we acquired last year.  We turned it around, had best Q4 ever.  People went from frightened, cynical, no trust in future of the business, to being optimistic.  One of their leaders a great example.  Had been managed by worst possible example of 'male German'.  She was v much a perfectionist herself.  He'd taught her fear - she wouldn't do anything unless she could make it perfect.  So CW spent time with her encouraging her to take risks, she's now transformed.  Project with Swiss airforce.  Shw said to CW she has a dev into AI and would love for him to work with us (CW / IR / me).  So he will, in March.  This is a huge difference - offering people out wasn't on the cards previously.
[Maybe 'Katrina'?]

Small team accountability is it.

Me: so giving her a sense of agency?
CW: Yes. Walking a tightrope somewhat, but I could offer someone else my trainers if I didn't need them - this is crudely the mindset.  So still focussed on your tightrope but open.  Businesses are naturally cautious of CW. It takes a while for them to realise it's a coaching / collaborative relationship - not friends, but trust does grow.

Another guy.  Someone who recently left a business we bought in South Africa.  Once worked at BetFred. 'Allergic to BAU'.  Product Manager type.  Probably going to work with us, possibly for free.  He helped turn around the SA business, now looking for next mission.  A few hours here and there for free for the heck of it. [This seems like very clear example of high engagement with Business Transformation projects at VG.]

VG $8-10bn today.

Every businesses only have 100-200 customers. So lots of focus.
CW: To be critical, our businesses sometimes struggle to understand the customer.  Tech-led, basically.  Not ridiculously so, but the problem comes up a lot.

Katrina. Example of toxic leadership above her.  Once held people in a hotel conference room until 2am until they agreed with a sales forecast nobody believed in.  Lied to markets etc.  VG comes in.  Trust issue from workforce because of toxic previous guys.  CW: by the end of the year, we replaced one leader quickly; another resigned, but we replaced internally; one hanging on for another 6-12 until retired; another we may replace; the rest are good; people surprisngly learning right way & doing right things.
CW: you cannot go in and tell them - persuading / showing / telling / reiterating.  With the people you start with, if they're competent, you should be able to bring along 60-70% on a short timeframe. You'll probably lose 20%.  If you go too low (bring 40% with you), then you aren't really building change.  And if it's too hight (90%), it's probably not really change.  After 6-12 months you look again and there's a genuine transformation.

This will apply to AI most likely.

Fear plays a big role in changes like this.

And as aside - hiring & onboarding is hard & expensive too. So change beats replacement of people in the general case.

VG has done almost 300 acquisitions so far.  Revenue has consistently grown over that time.

What's scary about the future.  We have to work out how to make things scale.

Perhaps looking at close to 500 by end of 2026 - so not linear scaling at all.

Me: So hockey-sticking by end of next year?
CW: Yeah. AI is well and truly in the middle of all that.'

Senior managers have embraced this journey.

North American VG businesses - 60% Canada, 40% US.

Average headcount 71, median 40.

$11.9m avg net rev, $7.3m median net rev.

Profit as a measure of health - not to milk the business because it's a good way of rooting out challenges, e.g. whether the product is healthy.

The three performance metrics VG cares most about:
BQR - 'business quality ratio'.
Maintenance
G&A
Then maybe 1 or 2 more considered important within each business.

R&D is considered the least useful - feels like a black box.  CW has approached Yonder about this previously [why them?].  Quite often R&D is then the first place businesses strip out to help them meet other targets.

So 2k businesses ultimately managed by perhaps 4 or 5 measures.

Talking now about leadership portal webpage...
'Jeff' was the architect for 'strategy insights'

'Initiatives' are pre-revenue projects we do within businesses - but because we have an investor mindset, we look for various proof points.  We try to give people pre-revenue funding from customers, and/or demonstrate genuine interest from customers first.  The advantage is you can do a multi-year investment, and it doesn't hurt thier day-to-day numbers.

Typically business leaders have portfolios named after their surname [I think I heart his correctly?]

Qaudrant Summary - bottom left (red) considered 'bad' - low profit / low growth.  We also dislike bottom right - high profit / low growth. It's a real challenge to get into the top right.  "It's a bit like getting a pea on a beachball."

When you look at portfolios in VG, you can see how they're split by quadrant.

Typically there are more businesses in the bottom left since we are scaling and we buy businesses that need improving.

[Q: where can I find a "Who's Who" of the org? Match people to portfolios? Etc.]

People in the top right are generally focussed on good pricing / adding new features / products.

Note that half of bonus is paid in shares.  You can choose to sell, though 80% of people (CW reckons) are happy to keep.

77% of VG BU leaders were in place at time of acquisition or have been internally promoted.

Acquiring managers are decentralised.  It's very unusual - AMs often compete with each other.  A lot of businesses we buy were 'poached' [internally] within 18 months of being purchased.  People can steal accounts from each other!  It's a conscious decision.

IT support is slow
